<span id="freeTextContainer15314770992455812584">How good Prince Caspian and his army of Talking beasts conquered the Telmarines.<br /><br />This edition follows the original numbering scheme. More recent publishers have re-numbered the volumes so that the books are ordered chronologically. This was reportedly the author's preference. Other editions number this book as #4.</span>

<div class="communityQuestionAndAnswer">
<div class="communityQuestion">
<div class="questionText">
<a href="/questions/551034-this-is-not-the-second-book-of-the">This is not the second book of the Chronicles of Narnia. Why is it labeled wrong?</a>
</div>
<div class="communityQAFooter questionFooter">
<ul class="questionFooterActions">
<li class="footerItem like">
<span id="like_container_community_question_551034" class="likeItContainer"><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F317501.Prince_Caspian" class="jsLike like_it" rel="nofollow">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp">
<a href="/questions/551034-this-is-not-the-second-book-of-the">3 months ago</a>
</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionPermalink">
<a href="/questions/551034-this-is-not-the-second-book-of-the">Add your answer</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class="allCommunityAnswers">
<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/47437530-colin" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/users/1446419082i/47437530._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" alt="" /></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/47437530-colin">Colin</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">It is the order in which they were originally published, it was only later that publishers decided to order them in chronological order of events,<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">It is the order in which they were originally published, it was only later that publishers decided to order them in chronological order of events, claiming that this was Lewis' own recommendation, although this is disputed (eg. by CS Lewis expert Alister Mcgrath thinks it is misleading to claim Lewis wanted people to read them in that order). <a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38136934" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/645979-amanda" class="left imgcol" title="Amanda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1327630769p2/645979.jpg" alt="Amanda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/38136934?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 19, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/645979-amanda" class="user" title="Amanda">Amanda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/645979-amanda?shelf=fer-teenagers-kids-and-younguns" class="actionLinkLite">fer-teenagers-kids-and-younguns</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/645979-amanda?shelf=fantasy-and-some-sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-and-some-sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/645979-amanda?shelf=2008" class="actionLinkLite">2008</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer38136934">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15373336712460757011">November 19, 2008. I've read these books a zillion and one times and surely I shall read them a zillion more. Because every single time, I realize new truths and find more honor in their pages. <br /><br />Today, I've read a passage that I find disturbing and quite out of character for CS Lewis: <br /><br />p.72<em> "Shall we go farther up for you, up to the crags? There's an Ogre or two and a Hag that we could introduce you to, up there." "Certainly not," said Caspian. "I should think not, indeed," said Trufflehunter. "We</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15373336712460757011">November 19, 2008. I've read these books a zillion and one times and surely I shall read them a zillion more. Because every single time, I realize new truths and find more honor in their pages. <br /><br />Today, I've read a passage that I find disturbing and quite out of character for CS Lewis: <br /><br />p.72<em> "Shall we go farther up for you, up to the crags? There's an Ogre or two and a Hag that we could introduce you to, up there." "Certainly not," said Caspian. "I should think not, indeed," said Trufflehunter. "We want none of that sort on our side."</em><br /><br />Seems a bit racist, if you ask me. It really makes me wonder exactly what CS Lewis is getting at here. It's totally the opposite of what happens in <em>The Last Battle</em> when Aslan sorts the good guys from the bad guys by whether they're good oir evil in their hearts. So anyway, it seems weird and I don't like it. The Hag does ends up being a bad guy in the end, but still... I dunno. <br /><br />I'll keep reading and blame the racism on the 1950s for now. <br /><br />Oh yeah, as a side note, whenever I read British literature, I talk to myself in a British accent and rhythm for a while afterward. It's so dorky!!!<br /><br />Later...<br />I've read a bit more now. The race issue didn't come up again. <br /><br />The battle scenes are not the same as you might see these days. There's something more frank and quick about them. Lewis doesn't explain every little move and maneuver, so in fact, if you're reading too fast, you might even miss a fight going on. Here's an example of a battle overview without much in the way of specifics:<br />P. 187<br /><em>But the new bout went well. Peter now seemed to be able to make some use of his shield, and he certainly made good use of his feet. He was almost playing Tig with Miraz now, keeping out of range shifting his ground, making the enemy work.</em><br /><br />I think if this book had been written today by a different author, it might be about 500 pages of battle scenes. I'm glad its not. Instead, the book is more about people standing on the side of good. Here's a passage that I just love which describes Edmund who may be a boy, but is also a king:<br />P.174<em>An hour later two great lords in the army of Miraz, the Lord Glozelle and the Lord Sopespian, strolling along their lines and picking their teeth after breakfast, looked up and saw coming down to them from the wood the Centaur and Giant Wembleweather, whom they had seen before in battle, and between them a figure they could not recognise. Nor indeed would the other boys at Edmund's school have recognised him if they could have seen him at that moment. For Aslan had breathed on him at their meeting and a kind of greatness hung about him. </em><br /><br />Ahhhhh... See? For Narnia and the North!<br /><br /><br />Also, you Tolkien fans will recognize the onslaught of trees which comes in at the end of the battle--Two Towers--and the river emerging (with the help of Bacchus and his grapevines) to take out the bridge and thwart the enemy in its path--Fellowship. Who came up with it first, I wonder... :)<br /><br /><br /><br />Later still...<br />As I finish reading this lovely little novel, allow me to <em>drop off to sleep with feet towards the fire and good friends on either side...</em> Thank you, Mr. Lewis. I <em> have</em> had a time. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15373336712460757011">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_712544570">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/712544570" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/14716035-bookwraiths" class="left imgcol" title="Bookwraiths"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1353765210p2/14716035.jpg" alt="Bookwraiths" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/712544570?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/14716035-bookwraiths" class="user" title="Bookwraiths">Bookwraiths</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1154894.Prince_Caspian" title="Audio 9781561797875" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/14716035-bookwraiths?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14716035-bookwraiths?shelf=audio-books" class="actionLinkLite">audio-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14716035-bookwraiths?shelf=young_adult" class="actionLinkLite">young_adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer712544570">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16451898214553213187">Reviewed at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://bookwraiths.wordpress.com/2013/09/19/prince-caspian-audio-book-good-fun/">Bookwraiths Reviews</a><br /><br /><em>Prince Caspian</em> is the second book in <em>The Chronicles of Narnia</em>. <br /><br />C.S. Lewis begins this tale by revisiting the Pevensie children, who have survived WW II and are at a train station waiting to head off to boarding school. While discussing their concerns about being separated, they are suddenly pulled into another world, which they do not immediately recognize as Narnia. Indeed, the land has changed to such an extent that it is only after finding several relics from th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16451898214553213187">Reviewed at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://bookwraiths.wordpress.com/2013/09/19/prince-caspian-audio-book-good-fun/">Bookwraiths Reviews</a><br /><br /><em>Prince Caspian</em> is the second book in <em>The Chronicles of Narnia</em>. <br /><br />C.S. Lewis begins this tale by revisiting the Pevensie children, who have survived WW II and are at a train station waiting to head off to boarding school. While discussing their concerns about being separated, they are suddenly pulled into another world, which they do not immediately recognize as Narnia. Indeed, the land has changed to such an extent that it is only after finding several relics from the past that they even begin to suspect that they are not only in Narnia but actually camped in the ruins of Cair Paravel: their former capital and home, where they reigned as high kings and queens of Narnia. <br /><br />Quickly, the siblings begin to understand that while only a small amount of time has passed in their world, many centuries have rolled by in their former home, which has resulted in the ruination of the castle and a changing of the very land itself. This new state of affairs is soon confirmed for the Pevensie children by one Trumpkin the dwarf, who they rescue from the Telmarines: the overlords of the new Narnia.<br /><br />What transpires after Trumpkin’s rescue is what I call the flashback story. Through Trumpkin, C.S. Lewis basically tells Peter, Edmund, Susan, and Lucy (and by default the reader/listener) all about the new Narnia and our title character, Prince Caspian. We hear about the invasion of the Telmarines, the fading of the old ways, the disappearance of the talking animals of Narnia, and the slow waning of all things magical in Narnia. But we also are told about the rightful heir of this new Narnia, Prince Caspian, who wishes to restore the land to its Golden Age when Peter, Edmund, Susan and Lucy ruled from Cair Paravel and who is even now in hiding with the Old Narnians, trying desperately to restore overthrow his wicked uncle and bring peace, prosperity, and magic back to the land.<br /><br />Only after hearing all this back story, does C.S. Lewis allow our four children to head out into the world on their grand adventure to aid Prince Caspian and thereby restore Narnia to its former glory.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book via audio book (which is a fairly new “reading” medium for me) and found the experience enjoyable and the actors’ performances well done. Specifically, this audio book did a great job of presenting the ambiance of Narnia’s different locals by description as well as sound effects, which on the whole livened up a fairly straightforward tale.<br /><br />As far as the story itself, I found myself conflicted on it: liking some things about it yet disliking others.<br /><br /><em>LIKES</em><br /><br />1) C.S. Lewis did not try to just rewrite <em>The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe</em>, but gives the reader a whole new Narnia experience. While the old Narnians - fauns, centaurs, talking animals, nymphs, living trees - are still around, they are now in hiding; driven to the edge of extinction by the Telmarines, who have not only conquered the land but turned it into a near non-magical world. This leads to a darker feeling to the story and allows a reader to see Peter, Edmund, Susan and Lucy both overjoyed at being back in Narnia but aghast at its desecration.<br /><br />2) Lewis allows the Pevensie children to actually grow up. Peter and Susan are shown as near adults, who are becoming blind to the magic in Narnia, while Edmund and Lucy rise to the forefront as the keepers of that magic. Especially illustrative of the “growth” of the characters were Lucy’s struggle to rediscover Aslan and Edmund’s stance as her steadfast supporter.<br /><br /><strong>DISLIKES</strong><br /><br />1) There just isn’t much suspense in this story. The children show up, rescue Trumpkin, get told all about what is going on then head out to join up with Prince Caspian. The majority of the story Peter, Edmund, Susan and Lucy really do not do anything, and even at the climax of the adventure, they are more bystanders than participants. Honestly, all the suspense and actions, which is described in the story, deal with Caspian and are “told” to us in flashback, not experienced as Caspian is living them. While I understand why C.S. Lewis crafted the story this way (The four children are a reader’s link to Narnia) I believe Caspian’s story itself would have been a more rousing tale.<br /><br />2) Things just work out too easily, even for what is obviously intended as a children’s story. For instance, Caspian grows up, becomes enamored with stories of ancient Narnia and up pops a half-dwarf tutor, who can provide all the lore Caspian needs. When he seeks refuge, the mysterious and little seen “old” Narnians turn up and take the Telmarine Prince into their hearts almost immediately. Each of these things seemed a bit rushed to me, but then again, it could be yet another draw back of flashback stories.<br /><br />All in all, this was an enjoyable listening experience, and much better than the movie - at least in my opinion. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16451898214553213187">...more</a>

          </span>

              <a href="/review/list/954028-deborah-markus?shelf=childhood-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">childhood-favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/954028-deborah-markus?shelf=modern-classics" class="actionLinkLite">modern-classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/954028-deborah-markus?shelf=comfort-books" class="actionLinkLite">comfort-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/954028-deborah-markus?shelf=coffee-break-for-my-brain" class="actionLinkLite">coffee-break-for-my-brain</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/954028-deborah-markus?shelf=trip-down-memory-lane" class="actionLinkLite">trip-down-memory-lane</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer752304308">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9339066297944565516">It's been years since I allowed myself the pleasure of rereading the Narnia books. And now I have two pleasures in reading these books: enjoying my old childhood joy, and analyzing the writing itself.<br /><br />One thing I remember noticing even as a child is the absolute dearth of femaleness. I don't mean female characters per se: in terms of having someone to care about and directly identify with, there's always a female child as well as a male one. (Everyone loves Lucy.) I mean that Lewis seems not to h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9339066297944565516">It's been years since I allowed myself the pleasure of rereading the Narnia books. And now I have two pleasures in reading these books: enjoying my old childhood joy, and analyzing the writing itself.<br /><br />One thing I remember noticing even as a child is the absolute dearth of femaleness. I don't mean female characters per se: in terms of having someone to care about and directly identify with, there's always a female child as well as a male one. (Everyone loves Lucy.) I mean that Lewis seems not to have understood that where there are sons, there must be mothers.<br /><br />(Belated warning: There will be spoilers. These books are over 60 years old, and there have been movies made of some of them. If you're over the age of 18 and you haven't read the Narnia books yet, clearly you have no plans to. Which I think is a shame, but hey, you're the boss of you.)<br /><br />Getting back to what I was saying about motherless sons: I'm speaking in a strictly biological sense, and boy <em>howdy</em> does Lewis seem to be squeamish about the strictly biological. When young prince Caspian is forced to flee into the wilderness, he befriends and allies himself with the talking beasts and magical creatures who have gone into hiding since the invasion of the Telmarines. (Excellent name, btw. Lewis is as gifted at naming as Dickens was.) <br /><br />In the course of this delightful search for what's left of the magical beings of Narnia, who does Caspian meet? There are dwarves and dwarf-folk -- descendants of dwarves who are part-human. All of these are male. It feels as if dwarves would <em>have</em> to marry humans in order to carry on the race, since we never, ever, <em>ever</em> hear <em>anything</em> about female dwarves. Probably because in Lewis' world, women are beautiful and good, or beautiful and evil, or ugly and evil; but they're never ugly (or even just ordinary-looking) and good. And the fact is, female dwarves wouldn't be gorgeous to human eyes. Lewis seems uncomfortable with this.<br /><br />(To be fair, so was his good friend, Tolkien. Terry Pratchett seems to be the first major writer to tackle the issue of female dwarves, and he does so delightfully in the Discworld novels.)<br /><br />So: lots of dwarves. All male. Who else? Three talking bears, all male. A giant squirrel, male. Glenstorm the centaur and his three sons. Um, male. Probably because centaurs are always portrayed as bare-chested rather than clothed, and Lewis goes along with that. Having a female centaur would mean having a topless woman or introducing the idea of centaur fashion, and Lewis seems uncomfortable with that. So we have a centaur with three male children and a wife who either died or keeps herself decently tucked away, which seems distinctly un-centaurlike. But okay. <br /><br />(The centaurs even offer refreshments to Caspian and his friends: oatcakes, and wine, and cheese. Boy-centaurs who cook are more okay to Lewis than female centaurs who do <em>anything.</em> Not that I'm bitter.) <br /><br />Who else? Twelve talking, fighting mice. All male. Several other talking animals, all male. (Unless Hogglestock the Hedgehog is female, which I sort of doubt, but I guess it's possible.) Fauns -- male. And a giant. Male.<br /><br />As I said, I remember wondering even as a child where the mothers and daughters and wives were. You kind of can't carry a species on without them. But Lewis is determined to manage somehow.<br /><br />You'd never know from reading this review so far that I <em>love</em> these books. I do. They're humorous and moving and just plain terrific stories. So many lines of dialogue have stuck with me over the years. I love it when Trumpkin the dwarf finds the Pevensie children and is awkwardly explaining that, well, he and his band of rebels had been hoping for some serious <em>military</em> help against the evil King Miraz when they decided to call back the four ancient rulers of Narnia: <br /><br /><em>"I suppose you are the four children out of the old stories," said Trumpkin. "And I'm very glad to meet you of course. And it's very interesting, no doubt. But -- no offence?" -- and he hesitated again.<br /><br />"Do get on and say whatever you're going to say," said Edmund.<br /><br />"Well then (no offence)," said Trumpkin. "But, you know, the King and Trufflehunter and Master Cornelius were expecting -- well, if you see what I mean, help. To put it in another way, I think they'd be imagining you as great warriors. As it is -- we're awfully fond of children and all that, but just at the moment, in the middle of a war -- but I'm sure you understand."</em><br /><br />The contest that follows, in which the children courteously invite the dwarf to compete with them in fencing and archery (and absolutely trounce him) is as delightful to read now as it was when I was a kid. It's <em>such</em> a joy to have a book in which children are active, competent, skillful characters. I think that's why these books continue to be read and enjoyed. Plenty has changed technologically in the years since they were written, but kids still love having the chance to be heroes. And the Pevensies are <em>ordinary</em> kids who get to do amazing things. <br /><br />Now: another thing I noticed this time around that I also puzzled over as a child was how odd some of Aslan's behavior is. Unless you specifically know that his rules and actions are a metaphor for Christianity, his choices are baffling. Why when he came to lead the children to the right path to reach Caspian did he put Lucy to the test that way? He tormented all of them with his elusiveness, and for what? <br /><br />Plot-wise, it makes no sense. If you know that it's "really" about having Christian faith, it clicks. Especially if you understand that some believers (certainly Lewis was one of them) are firmly convinced that people who say they don't believe in the Christian God secretly do, in their deepest heart of hearts. Here's Susan, talking to Lucy about Aslan:<br /><br /><em>"I really believed it was him -- he, I mean -- yesterday. When he warned us not to go down to the fir-wood. And I really believed it was him tonight, when you woke us up. I mean, deep down inside. Or I could have, if I'd let myself. But I just wanted to get out of the woods and -- and -- oh, I don't know."</em><br /><br />I don't know either. I'm too straightforward to be able to make much sense of that sort of thing. <br /><br />Again, these stories are strong enough to stand up to their own weaknesses. I loved them then and I love them now. But it's fun to take a keener look at them now that I'm a so-called grownup.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9339066297944565516">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10579150055680979468"><em>Stephen wrote: "Keep in mind that Lewis' mother died young, he had no sisters, and he married compartively late in life -- four years after this book</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10579150055680979468"><em>Stephen wrote: "Keep in mind that Lewis' mother died young, he had no sisters, and he married compartively late in life -- four years after this book was published, in fact. In reading his autobiography, there are no females playing a major role in his life -- only his father, brother, the other boys at boarding school, teachers, then more men in the WW1 trenches. Isaac Asimov confessed the same weakness: not having any experience around women, he preferred not writing them at all to writing them badly. Not until he married did Asimov produce a female main character."</em><br /><br />Good to know, and thanks for pointing this out. <br /><br />But surely bears, mice, and hedgehogs wouldn't exactly be "women" if they were presented as female characters. All you have to do is give them feminine-sounding names and then let them run around being bears, mice, etc.<br /><br />And it's now extra ironic that Lucy is probably his most memorable and deservedly best-loved character -- and is every bit as active as either of her brothers (arguably more so).</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer273939410">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6778168198064893822">Note: Just finished reading this one with my 5 yr old, and we loved it! I'm enjoying it much better this time around. Maybe because I didn't understand all the implications the first time, or maybe because I'm seeing it through the eyes of my child. Either way, I love C.S. Lewis more each time I read him! And I've decided not to tell my son about the analogy to Christianity. I want him to figure it out for himself. But just the other day he compared God to Aslan and Spiderman all in the same sen</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6778168198064893822">Note: Just finished reading this one with my 5 yr old, and we loved it! I'm enjoying it much better this time around. Maybe because I didn't understand all the implications the first time, or maybe because I'm seeing it through the eyes of my child. Either way, I love C.S. Lewis more each time I read him! And I've decided not to tell my son about the analogy to Christianity. I want him to figure it out for himself. But just the other day he compared God to Aslan and Spiderman all in the same sentence! <br /><br /><br />This is the review I wrote for the overall series:<br /><br />The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe by CS Lewis is one of the books in his series, the Chronicles of Narnia in which Christianity is portrayed through various fantasy creatures. God, for instance is portrayed as a talking Lion. What a wonderful series! What child hasn’t climbed into a closet and explored the back cracks in hope of finding an entrance to a new and exciting world after reading this book? I used to sit in a closet with the door closed and a flashlight reading my favorite books after reading this series, in hopes that someday a door would open and take me to another realm. Of course, the white witch is my favorite character. I’m always attracted to the bad ones. The Lion, Aslan, is a wonderful character as well, but I have to admit, knowing that he was an analogy for God, changed my view of the story a bit and left me a bit disappointed. He was a bit cheesy. Or maybe typical is a better word. Which is why I almost wish I wouldn’t have known the true meaning of the books until after I read them. In any case, the stories were great, the first one being the best. (You always lose a little of the naiveté of the children as they get older) But the movies did them justice as well. Reading them again as an adult, found me a little bored, but still enchanted overall with the series. The next movie is due out soon and I can only hope they will continue to make the movies which were incredible. I highly recommend this series and consider it a classic as well.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://classicsdefined.com/narnia.html"> ClassicsDefined.com</a></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1286516623?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 22, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/25327841-p-z" class="user" title="P.Z.">P.Z.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a href="/review/list/25327841-p-z?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>,

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_923790777">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/923790777" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/30718608-brian" class="left imgcol" title="Brian "><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1434163558p2/30718608.jpg" alt="Brian " /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/923790777?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 09, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/30718608-brian" class="user" title="Brian ">Brian </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/28868.Prince_Caspian" title="Hardcover 9780061125256" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=childhood-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">childhood-favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=read-twice" class="actionLinkLite">read-twice</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=read-3x" class="actionLinkLite">read-3x</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=2015" class="actionLinkLite">2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/30718608-brian?shelf=christian" class="actionLinkLite">christian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer923790777">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12100722730103229938"> I enjoyed this book more than I thought I would. I’m starting to adapt to Lewis’s style. Before I thought he lacked writing skills because he isn’t like other authors I’ve read, but I’m starting to see his uniqueness.<br /><br /> Peter, Edmond, Susan and Lucy are called back to Narnia by the Horn Susan left behind. In the last book, they grew to be older and famous Kings and Queens, then came back to their own world as children. A thousand years has passed in Narnia, and a single year in our world. People</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12100722730103229938"> I enjoyed this book more than I thought I would. I’m starting to adapt to Lewis’s style. Before I thought he lacked writing skills because he isn’t like other authors I’ve read, but I’m starting to see his uniqueness.<br /><br /> Peter, Edmond, Susan and Lucy are called back to Narnia by the Horn Susan left behind. In the last book, they grew to be older and famous Kings and Queens, then came back to their own world as children. A thousand years has passed in Narnia, and a single year in our world. People don’t believe like they did, in Aslan and Fawns and Dryads and Centaurs and Tree-Spirits and all the mythical creature of Narnia. A new man has taken charge, Miraz, by deception. Prince Caspian remains the true heir of Narnia, and he flees for his life. The four children help Caspian gain his rightful place, and restore the faith the land has lost.<br /><br /> I had a “revelation,” if you will, reading this. I’ve listened to Madeleine L’Engle speak on YouTube of deeper truths. I’ve heard theories of fantasy; they point to truths beyond our reasoning minds. I started to remember the pleasure I felt a child reading this. When we grow older, we become hardened because our view of the world and our paradigm of life become progressively narrower as we age. Reading about these awesome creatures and this other world and this mysterious Lion made me realize the existence of deeper truths I know not yet. That makes life exciting. Some truths go beyond the mind, beyond rationale, beyond the teaching of spiritual leaders and even texts and books explaining religious beliefs. Some of these truths only come alive through fantasy, through the imagination, through the abandonment of reality and the structure of the rules that govern our world. For me, this excited me to know there is more about my spiritual life than I can get from a textbook or a theology lesson. It’s deeper, more alive, more beautiful than dry theology, and dead religion.<br /><br /> I’m fast becoming a fan of C.S. Lewis. I didn’t believe this would happen. <br /><br /> It’s happening. <br /><br /> In the end Aslan tells Peter he won't be able to return because he's getting too old. May I never become too "old" to return to Narnia.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12100722730103229938">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2823522412028647479">I love your happy version better than mine. I tend to see things from the half empty glass side and it makes me wonder why I always choose this route. I will say that Peter at the end seems to be implying that although he's been banned from returning due to age, in his heart he'll forever be able to return to Narnia when he wants. So perhaps it's a little of both our interpretations. (Keep in mind this is coming from someone who's never read the book, so I could be way off base.)<br /><br />All of the above though just reminds me of the moment you come to the realization that you are no longer a child. It is almost like something dies inside you. We all return to those moments at times, but in general we seem to grow a veil over our eyes and it's harder for most of us to see things through rosy color eyes.</span>

Not to mention where the fuck is Aslan???? Plus, Prince Caspian's stupidity amazes me to no end. <br /><br />This book <strong>bores</strong> me thoroughly with its oh-so-fascinating plot. <br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1409510310ra/10987401.gif" /><br /><br /><em>Ugh!</em> As if the good guys wouldn't win at the end and everyone <s>fuck you Pevensies</s> lives happily ever after, that is until the next installment.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText2961995179081014459">So, I'm just going to come out and say this book <strong>sucks</strong>.<br /><br />I hate almost every character in this book, especially the Pevensies <s>except for Edmund who really turned out good</s>. Not to mention where the fuck is Aslan???? Plus, Prince Caspian's stupidity amazes me to no end. <br /><br />This book <strong>bores</strong> me thoroughly with its oh-so-fascinating plot. <br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1409510310ra/10987401.gif" /><br /><br /><em>Ugh!</em> As if the good guys wouldn't win at the end and everyone <s>fuck you Pevensies</s> lives happily ever after, that is until the next installment. I know that this is a common theme in every famous YA series there is, but could this book at least give us some heart-wrenching moments to look forward/react to?? <s>The answer's NO btw.</s><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1409511683ra/10987604.gif" /><br /><br />Reading this actually gave me real pain, <s>and I'm not talking about the sweet, delicious kind *Rawrr*</s>. It is sleep-inducing and yawn-worthy at best, NOT my cup of tea. Since the first chapter, I was already feeling the boredom weighing down on me and finishing it was an over-achievement. <br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1409529440ra/10989643.gif" /><br /><br />Even though this book is considered a 'wonderful' classic. <div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_131470879">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/131470879" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="left imgcol" title="K.D. Absolutely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449978413p2/2156200.jpg" alt="K.D. Absolutely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/131470879?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 02, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="user" title="K.D. Absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=series" class="actionLinkLite">series</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=british" class="actionLinkLite">british</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer131470879">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13297486001641159308">My fourth Narnia book and this one is pure fantasy to me. I missed the Christian allegory that the earlier books, particularly <a title="The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe (Chronicles of Narnia, #2) by C.S. Lewis" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/100915.The_Lion__the_Witch__and_the_Wardrobe__Chronicles_of_Narnia___2_">The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe</a> (3 stars) has. <br /><br />The book is about the return of the four kids, Peter, Susan, Edmund and Lucy to Narnia after 1,300 years in Narnian time. However, this is just about a year later in London time. It is the horn of Susan that she has left when the kids are leaving Narnia in Book 1 that is used by Prince Caspian, the rightful heir of the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13297486001641159308">My fourth Narnia book and this one is pure fantasy to me. I missed the Christian allegory that the earlier books, particularly <a title="The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe (Chronicles of Narnia, #2) by C.S. Lewis" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/100915.The_Lion__the_Witch__and_the_Wardrobe__Chronicles_of_Narnia___2_">The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe</a> (3 stars) has. <br /><br />The book is about the return of the four kids, Peter, Susan, Edmund and Lucy to Narnia after 1,300 years in Narnian time. However, this is just about a year later in London time. It is the horn of Susan that she has left when the kids are leaving Narnia in Book 1 that is used by Prince Caspian, the rightful heir of the kingdom of Narnia that is by then ruled by Telmarines who has conquered Narnia. When the prince's life is endangered he blows the horn and the kids are mysteriously pulled out <em>ala-Harry Potter</em> from the London subway station.<br /><br />Just like the earlier three book, this one is an easy read. However, it sometimes felt like boring maybe because I already knew what would happen because I earlier saw the movie adaptation. Also, maybe I was too busy with my job and other matters that I had no patience for fantasy children's books nowadays. I only picked this up because a good friend of mine bought a whole set of Narnia and he gushed about how the first two books made him fall in love with the series. I am not damping his spirits by giving this a two-star rating. It's just that we have a two-decade difference in age and if he still revels and is drawn to excitement by magics and flying dragons, I have long ago stopped believing on those.<br /><br />Still, Narnia is Lewis' answer to his friend Tolkien's Middle Earth and nobody can criticize the brilliance of these two prominent British authors. I am still geared up towards reading the whole series like how I had the patience and enjoyment in finishing the whole LOTR series last year.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13297486001641159308">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_303471916">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/303471916" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5105002-peter" class="left imgcol" title="Peter"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448640054p2/5105002.jpg" alt="Peter" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/303471916?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5105002-peter" class="user" title="Peter">Peter</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/121749.Prince_Caspian" title="Hardcover 9780007202300" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5105002-peter?shelf=middle-grade" class="actionLinkLite">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5105002-peter?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5105002-peter?shelf=read-2012" class="actionLinkLite">read-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer303471916">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer81204379177089161">I think I preferred the movie version, it was much better plotted than the book, eschewing the book's flashback structure and sitting around for a parallel stories that ran together with a lot more action and excitement. The characters – the Penvensies (who I cannot stand in the books) and Caspian (who is little more than a cypher) – had some facets in the film where as in the book they are totally one dimensional. I don't particularly like the insidious black-and-white morality that pervades ev</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText81204379177089161">I think I preferred the movie version, it was much better plotted than the book, eschewing the book's flashback structure and sitting around for a parallel stories that ran together with a lot more action and excitement. The characters – the Penvensies (who I cannot stand in the books) and Caspian (who is little more than a cypher) – had some facets in the film where as in the book they are totally one dimensional. I don't particularly like the insidious black-and-white morality that pervades everything, the undertones of sexism, racism and classism. The laboured moral lessons slapped in the middle of each story, here about faith, Lucy is the only one who can see Aslan etc. There's also a mixing of cosy fireside-ness (the bolgy bears??) with war treated as a cricket match, where the private school heroes beat the oiks. There's a weird mixing of Roman god Bacchus with Aslan where they all dance through towns righting wrongs, and generally having a bacchanal – but obviously a very morally respectable and restrained one. Oh, and there are also walking trees – like Ents, but then I guess this and LOTR were written at the same time in the same group. A lot of this is so tonally wacky you can see why they dropped it from the film in favour of the main adventure story which I think they strengthened to great effect. So far my least favourite Narnia book, although still three to go.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="81204379177089161">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer33056741">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8942548013522474526">3.0 to 3.5 stars. This is the kind of YA fantasy story that is great to read with the kids as the good guys are really good and the bad guys are really bad. The plot and dialogue are a bit too YA for me to give this a higher rating but I certainly admire the world that C.S. Lewis created and will definitely finish the chronicles. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_40840368">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40840368" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1552211-tina" class="left imgcol" title="Tina"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1333881094p2/1552211.jpg" alt="Tina" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/40840368?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 31, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1552211-tina" class="user" title="Tina">Tina</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/636650.Prince_Caspian" title="Paperback 9780590254786" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=2010" class="actionLinkLite">2010</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=ya" class="actionLinkLite">ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer40840368">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16777216380746625272"><strong>Original post at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://onemorepage.tinamats.com/prince-caspian">One More Page</a></strong><br /><br />When the movie <strong> <em>Prince Caspian</em> </strong> came out, I watched it without having read the book, so I had zero expectations. All I thought after I watched the movie was it was a little bit long, and I squeed when Aslan showed up. I didn't really like it as much as the first movie on the first watch but it got better when I watched it for the second and third time. Eventually, <strong> <em>Prince Caspian</em> </strong> became one of those movies that I like watching over and over again, despite my friend</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16777216380746625272"><strong>Original post at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://onemorepage.tinamats.com/prince-caspian">One More Page</a></strong><br /><br />When the movie <strong>
  <em>Prince Caspian</em>
</strong> came out, I watched it without having read the book, so I had zero expectations. All I thought after I watched the movie was it was a little bit long, and I squeed when Aslan showed up. I didn't really like it as much as the first movie on the first watch but it got better when I watched it for the second and third time. Eventually, <strong>
  <em>Prince Caspian</em>
</strong> became one of those movies that I like watching over and over again, despite my friends' complaints of it not being faithful to the book, etc, etc.<br /><br />But how many times have we learned that movies are never equal to the books, and that Hollywood will always, always change something in the book for reasons we do not know and still get angry about?<br /><br />Anyway, so I finally read <em>Prince Caspian</em> just before the year ended. From the initial impressions of my friends who read the book before watching the movie, I was prepared to see glaring differences compared to the movie. I wasn't sure what I'd like more, of course, but I've learned to read with an open mind.<br /><br />I was surprised to find out that there really wasn't much difference. Well, okay, it <em>is</em> different in terms of how the story flowed, and how the sequences were made and how dark it feels and the romance (yes, it never existed in the book). But I can see why the movie people diverged from the book. <em>Prince Caspian</em> is not really an exciting book. While there was a battle, many struggles for both Caspian and the Pevensies, and even some black magic, the way it was written just doesn't shine as much as <em><a rel="nofollow" href="http://onemorepage.tinamats.com/the-lion-the-witch-and-the-wardrobe" title="The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe">The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</a>.</em> A Lewis fan and friend told me that Caspian is the book he tends to skim over, and while I didn't really skim through it, it did prove to be very fast reading as I got through 3/4 of it while I was getting my hair done in the salon. Then I stopped so it took me a while to actually finish it.<br /><br />But I'm not saying that <em>Prince Caspian</em> is a bad book. It just wasn't exciting as its predecessor.* There were no extravagant rescues, there were no betrayals or resurrections. My favorite part, as always, was when Aslan showed up. It wasn't quite like how it was done in the movies, but I liked how Lewis wrote it so that the other Pevensies didn't see Aslan immediately because of the fear that was in their hearts. That's the same in our faith walk, don't you think? We can only see God when we let our fears go. And once we see Him, things will never be the same.<br /><br />There's this one passage in the book that I really loved, because it sounded so poetic and beautiful:<br /><blockquote>But all night, Aslan and the Moon gazed upon each other with joyful and unblinking eyes. <em>(p. 213)</em>
</blockquote><br /><strong>
  <em>Prince Caspian</em>
</strong> is a good book. Perhaps I'd learn to appreciate it more when I re-read it, but it certainly made me appreciate the movie more. I think it's time for another re-watch. :)<br /><br /><em>*<strong>NOTE:</strong> I must say that I am reading the Narnia books in order of publication, so when I say predecessor, I meant <strong>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe </strong>and not <strong>The Horse and His Boy</strong>. </em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16777216380746625272">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer319936683424892822">I just wanted to call attention to what a profound lesson it is when Lucy hears and sees Aslan and the others don't. The response of the others very much parallels the way people respond sometimes when you KNOW you are following the Lord to undertake a certain endeavor.<br /><br />Some, like Susan will tell you you're always dreaming and go back to bed.<br /><br />Some, like Edmund will point out that you've been right before and stick up for you even though he can't quite see what you are suggesting.<br /><br />Some, like Peter w</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText319936683424892822">I just wanted to call attention to what a profound lesson it is when Lucy hears and sees Aslan and the others don't. The response of the others very much parallels the way people respond sometimes when you KNOW you are following the Lord to undertake a certain endeavor.<br /><br />Some, like Susan will tell you you're always dreaming and go back to bed.<br /><br />Some, like Edmund will point out that you've been right before and stick up for you even though he can't quite see what you are suggesting.<br /><br />Some, like Peter will admit that you are probably right but then let the pragmatic, circumstantial side of things dictate and kill your trust in God.<br /><br />Some, like Trumpkin the dwarf are completely blind and so have no idea what you're talking about.<br /><br />But the blessed thing is that when you follow Aslan anyway, often the others around you end up being willing to try what you are saying. <br /><br />There is a good page here if you happen to live in a family that doesn't go to church. Just begin going yourself. Who knows, the others may follow!<br /><br />Also, these chapters are precious encouragement if you feel like you haven't really connected with Jesus lately.<br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_679150973">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/679150973" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9102458-carissa" class="left imgcol" title="Carissa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448945089p2/9102458.jpg" alt="Carissa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/679150973?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 30, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9102458-carissa" class="user" title="Carissa">Carissa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/587316.Prince_Caspian" title="Paperback 9780064471053" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9102458-carissa?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9102458-carissa?shelf=kids-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">kids-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9102458-carissa?shelf=read-in-2013" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer679150973">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13748713289938721428">"Bother!" said Edmund, "I've left my new torch in Narnia."<br /><br />I can't count the number of times I've read this book, but it grows dearer to me with each reading. Like rediscovering a gem I'd half-forgotten. That's the beauty of Narnia.<br /><br />In <em>Prince Caspian</em>, the book follows Peter, Susan, Edmund, and Lucy as they are called back to Narnia, this time from the blowing of Susan's magic horn by the desperate Prince Caspian as he fights alongside the Old Narnians against his Uncle Miraz. One of the best aspec</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13748713289938721428">"Bother!" said Edmund, "I've left my new torch in Narnia."<br /><br />I can't count the number of times I've read this book, but it grows dearer to me with each reading. Like rediscovering a gem I'd half-forgotten. That's the beauty of Narnia.<br /><br />In <em>Prince Caspian</em>, the book follows Peter, Susan, Edmund, and Lucy as they are called back to Narnia, this time from the blowing of Susan's magic horn by the desperate Prince Caspian as he fights alongside the Old Narnians against his Uncle Miraz. One of the best aspects of this book is how the timeline is so inventively written. We learn about Caspian, but only halfway through the book, after the children are called from England and have no idea why they are in Narnia. It's a fantastic and clever design and one more writers should use. Playing with the timeline, when done right, makes a story much more interesting.<br /><br />It's possible that people can read about Narnia without understanding the complexity of its allegory. But where's the fun in missing half the point? C. S. Lewis knew how to be faithful to his beliefs in his writing without cramming it down anyone's throat. As a Christian, I know who Aslan represents, and his interactions with the children when they disappoint him, still so filled with love, is telling of Christ's interactions with his children. It's beautiful and I think it's profound in how Lucy is the only one who first sees Aslan in <em>Prince Caspian</em>. Perhaps because she was looking for him. It's a beautiful book, one I read to my sister before she could read chapter books, and one I hope to read to my children should the Lord bless me in that way.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13748713289938721428">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/4695-daniel" class="left imgcol" title="Daniel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1264806364p2/4695.jpg" alt="Daniel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/65347478?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 31, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4695-daniel" class="user" title="Daniel">Daniel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4695-daniel?shelf=2009" class="actionLinkLite">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer65347478">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7959690749577930266">Here's one thing I don't get about the parts of "The Chronicles of Narnia" I've read so far: Why does C.S. Lewis feel the need, in each book, to make one of the Pevensie children, seemingly at random, completely loathsome? In "The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe," it was Edmund who was a complete dick for almost the entire book, and now, in "Prince Caspian," it's Susan who's asking for a good bitch-slapping.<br /><br />I think I know why C.S. Lewis does this: He's not very good at making characters memorab</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7959690749577930266">Here's one thing I don't get about the parts of "The Chronicles of Narnia" I've read so far: Why does C.S. Lewis feel the need, in each book, to make one of the Pevensie children, seemingly at random, completely loathsome? In "The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe," it was Edmund who was a complete dick for almost the entire book, and now, in "Prince Caspian," it's Susan who's asking for a good bitch-slapping.<br /><br />I think I know why C.S. Lewis does this: He's not very good at making characters memorable unless he makes them totally good, totally bad, or start out totally bad and have them turn totally good partway through the story. I know these are children's books, but even children's books can have a little bit of complexity in their characterization, no?<br /><br />Just as in "Lion," none of the characters in "Caspian" much deserve to remain in the reader's mind after the book is closed. The possible exception is Trumpkin, and he stands out mostly by using such exclamations as "lobsters and lollipops" and "giants and junipers." That's just funny. Caspian himself starts out with the potential to become interesting too, but largely fades into the background once the Pevensie children return to Narnia.<br /><br />Getting back to Susan, I do get what C.S. Lewis is trying to do with her character in "Caspian." This is still a religious parable, after all, and in this book Susan is the one designated to stop trusting in Aslan, just as someone straying from the Christian faith will stop following Jesus. Fine, no problem, I get it. But Lewis never bothers telling us <em>why</em> Susan has strayed. Did she develop some sort of alcohol problem between the first and second books? Lose her boyfriend in a fiery car crash? Come on, Jack, give us something. You could at least have had one of Aslan's enemies offer Susan thirty pieces of Turkish delight for the lion's head or something. I just want some motivation, for God's sake.<br /><br />That all being said, the story in "Caspian" is fast-moving and entertaining, and, just as in "Lion," the writing is lively and engaging. Moving on to "The Voyage of the Dawn Treader."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7959690749577930266">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_24163651">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24163651" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/113969-aili" class="left imgcol" title="Aili"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1180926096p2/113969.jpg" alt="Aili" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/24163651?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/113969-aili" class="user" title="Aili">Aili</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer24163651">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14131264482359671045">Read this in 2 hours the day I went to see the movie. I had forgotten pretty much everything about this book. Re-reading it, I can see why I didn't remember anything (and why this is my least favorite Narnia book) -- it's because nothing happens. Most of the actual drama (how Caspian's uncle took the throne; Caspian's education and eventual escape) takes place in flashback. What we do get is a lot of faith, or lack of faith, in Aslan coming to save the day. Wah wah wah. The final conflict is ant</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14131264482359671045">Read this in 2 hours the day I went to see the movie. I had forgotten pretty much everything about this book. Re-reading it, I can see why I didn't remember anything (and why this is my least favorite Narnia book) -- it's because nothing happens. Most of the actual drama (how Caspian's uncle took the throne; Caspian's education and eventual escape) takes place in flashback. What we do get is a lot of faith, or lack of faith, in Aslan coming to save the day. Wah wah wah. The final conflict is anticlimactic, and the denouement is really rushed (like <a title=" C.S. Lewis" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20C.S.%20Lewis"> C.S. Lewis</a> was late to catch a plane or something).<br /><br />On the other hand, the movie version was less of a let-down because I had recently been presented with the mediocrity of the book. So directorial choices like having Caspian and Peter get into a "who-is-king" pissing contest, while irritating, were more understandable.<br /><br />Re-reading this was useful in reminding myself of the difference between children's and young adult literature. Even though this is a chapter book, it probably works best as a story for littler kids. I think in the first go-round, the entire Narnia series was read aloud by my mom (with the exception of <a title=" The Last Battle" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20Last%20Battle"> The Last Battle</a> -- too much Jesus). I had forgotten that, and was remembering them more as teen fantasy lit, like <a title=" Alanna: The First Adventure" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20Alanna:%20The%20First%20Adventure"> Alanna: The First Adventure</a> or the initial trilogy of Valdemar books by <a title=" Mercedes Lackey" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20Mercedes%20Lackey"> Mercedes Lackey</a>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14131264482359671045">...more</a>

  <div class="xhr_comment_photo">
    	<a href="/user/show/2246621-dana-salman" alt="Dana Salman"><img alt="2246621" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1438609097p1/2246621.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="xhr_comment_body">
    <a href="/user/show/2246621-dana-salman" style="font-weight:bold">Dana Salman</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer3954609677857606289">You are so right. I saw the movie in cinema before I read the book. The book really disappointed me, and I could see why they had to add some things i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3954609677857606289">You are so right. I saw the movie in cinema before I read the book. The book really disappointed me, and I could see why they had to add some things in the movie, like the part where they try to infiltrate the Telmerine's castle. I liked the rivalry between Caspian and Peter, that put alot more passion into the movie. In the book they were as friendly as anything. Because in the book, they don't make any reference to the fact that the four kings and queens of Narnia were once adults and were treated like royalty, and are now once again just plain old kids who get pushed around. Being a kid, Peter starts to act a little childish, and he starts arguing with Caspian, who in the movie is a bit older than him. In the book, he picks up on his king-ness a little too fast. After spending a year as a child, you'd think Peter would act a little bratty, but he didn't. Then again, you don't want a tantrum maker for your hero. That would make the whole thing look like a joke. So we're stuck with the conclusion that C.S Lewis had to make Peter the ideal High King once more, and let's face it, noble kings are boring. What would've stopped the story from being boring was if there had been a little more leadership from Peter to Edmund, who was young and foolish in the second book of the series, and has matured a bit to see Peter's flaws. He is a better judge of character and a better fighter than he had once been. he became my favorite character in the movie, despite what a bastard he was in the prequel, yet his role as the younger less important brother is barely seen in the book Prince Caspian, because that motif had already been made in the Lion, The Witch, and The Wardrobe. And using a motif in a sequel will give people the impression that you're just rewriting the whole story.<br />What Lewis is left with, then, is, as you've said before, nothing. Nothing happens.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3954609677857606289">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/210634400?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 11, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5735930-cary" class="user" title="Cary">Cary</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/587316.Prince_Caspian" title="Paperback 9780064471053" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5735930-cary?shelf=christian-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">christian-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5735930-cary?shelf=owned" class="actionLinkLite">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer210634400">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16828227941025327056">I remember the first time I learned about the chronicles of narnia was when one of my professors back in college asked us, as a bonus question in our exam, to enumerate the seven books of the series. During that time, i've only heard about TLTWTW so instead of feeling happy for the bonus points, i got disappointed for i've only one point for that bonus question.(Actually, the exam was so hard that i have to rely on the bonus questions in order to get a passing score.) Fortunately, i was able to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16828227941025327056">I remember the first time I learned about the chronicles of narnia was when one of my professors back in college asked us, as a bonus question in our exam, to enumerate the seven books of the series. During that time, i've only heard about TLTWTW so instead of feeling happy for the bonus points, i got disappointed for i've only one point for that bonus question.(Actually, the exam was so hard that i have to rely on the bonus questions in order to get a passing score.) Fortunately, i was able to pass the exam even without the bonus but that question remained in me forever that i became curious about the series so i made a promised to myself that i will read those books someday.<br /><br />It was only last year though that i've started fulfilling that promise by reading the first three books of the series and so far, Lewis has never failed to fascinate me with his works that i did not hesitate to buy the set when i saw it at the UP christmas bazzar. <br /><br />Okay, enough for that long introduction. Let me now give justice to that 5 stars.<br /><br />This fourth book of the Narnia series is actually about the return of the pevensie siblings to narnia a year after their return to earth from that magical wardrobe. To their surprise, it seems that a year on earth is equivalent to a thousand years or so in narnia so what they found were only the remains of the kingdom that had once belong to them. They later found out that the reason why they were brought back to narnia was that a rightful heir of the throne is in danger of losing his crown. So once again, the children have to carry out their mission and restore the old narnian kingdom.<br />I have actually seen the movie prior to reading this book but im glad that the scenes from the movie seem so vague to me so was able to appreciate Lewis' account of the story. The movie is also different from the book with the love angle between Caspian and Susan, which is actually absent in the book, so that was an added point to the book.<br /><br />Actually the reason why i love this book is the underlying theme of the story. More than the victory they've gained at the end of the story, i love how the hope of the real narnians rise up upon knowing that Aslan is alive for he seems so distant for a long time. I also love how the author tied up lose ends and answer the question i have in mind in the beginning of the book - Where did those Telmarines came from? <br /><br />I think the only distraction i had while reading this was that i cant help but interchangeably imagine the scenes from the lord of the rings movie with all those moving trees.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16828227941025327056">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_17989477">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17989477" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/883725-julianna" class="left imgcol" title="Julianna"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442976820p2/883725.jpg" alt="Julianna" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/17989477?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 03, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/883725-julianna" class="user" title="Julianna">Julianna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/883725-julianna?shelf=read-2008" class="actionLinkLite">read-2008</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/883725-julianna?shelf=tween-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">tween-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/883725-julianna?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer17989477">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7061852298162951094">I read <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em> when I was in 5th or 6th grade, but for some reason, never chose to finish the series. With <em>The Chronicles of Narnia</em> finally being made into movies, I decided it was time to rectify that situation, since I have always had a preference for reading the book before seeing the movie. Though I didn't find it to be quite as compelling a story as <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em>, <em>Prince Caspian</em> was still a very good follow-up. It was a little like visitin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7061852298162951094">I read <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em> when I was in 5th or 6th grade, but for some reason, never chose to finish the series. With <em>The Chronicles of Narnia</em> finally being made into movies, I decided it was time to rectify that situation, since I have always had a preference for reading the book before seeing the movie. Though I didn't find it to be quite as compelling a story as <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em>, <em>Prince Caspian</em> was still a very good follow-up. It was a little like visiting with old friends. I enjoyed learning a bit more about the history of Narnia, and seeing how it had changed since the Pevensie children had ruled the land. It was also very nice to see them reconnect with Aslan and once again wrest Narnia from the control of evildoers, though I have to say that the "bad guys" in this story just simply weren't as convincing as the White Witch in <em>The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe</em>. I must admit though that I really loved the scenes where the girls, along with many of the creatures and inhabitants of Narnia have a feast, as well as the celebration everyone took part in following their victory. All the joy and happiness in those parts of the narrative just really drew me into the story. Although I was left with some questions that I hope will be answered in the remaining books in the series, I definitely thought <em>Prince Caspian</em> was a fun and enjoyable read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7061852298162951094">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
